<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">In this <a href="/wiki/Chinese_name" title="Chinese name">Chinese name</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_surname" title="Chinese surname">family name</a> is <i> <a href="/wiki/Sh%C4%9Bn" title="Shěn">Shen</a></i>.</div>

System">Tâi-lô</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Min Nan Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="nan-Latn" style="font-style: normal">Sím Kuat</span></span></td></tr></tbody></table></td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Shen Kuo</b> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">沈括</span>; 1031–1095) or <b>Shen Gua</b>, <a href="/wiki/Courtesy_name" title="Courtesy name">courtesy name</a> <b>Cunzhong</b> (存中) and <a href="/wiki/Art_name#China" title="Art name">pseudonym</a> <b>Mengqi</b> (now usually given as <b>Mengxi</b>) <b>Weng</b> (夢溪翁),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yao_2003_544_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-yao_2003_544-1">[1]</a></sup> was a Chinese <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymathic</a> scientist and statesman of the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> (960–1279). Excelling in many fields of study and statecraft, he was a <a href="/wiki/Chinese_mathematics" title="Chinese mathematics">mathematician</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_astronomy" title="Chinese astronomy">astronomer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antiquarian" title="Antiquarian">antiquarian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meteorologist" title="Meteorologist">meteorologist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geologist" title="Geologist">geologist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Entomologist" title="Entomologist">entomologist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomist" title="Anatomist">anatomist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climatologist" title="Climatologist">climatologist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoologist" title="Zoologist">zoologist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Botanist" title="Botanist">botanist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">pharmacologist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_scientist" title="Medical scientist">medical scientist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agronomist" title="Agronomist">agronomist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeologist" title="Archaeologist">archaeologist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnographer" title="Ethnographer">ethnographer</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartographer" title="Cartographer">cartographer</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geomorphologist" title="Geomorphologist">geomorphologist</a>,  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geomagnetics" title="Geomagnetics">geomagnetician</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">metallurgist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mineralogist" title="Mineralogist">mineralogist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Encyclopedist" title="Encyclopedist">encyclopedist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_history_of_China_(pre-1911)" title="Military history of China (pre-1911)">military general</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Imperial_China" title="Foreign relations of Imperial China">diplomat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydraulics" title="Hydraulics">hydraulic engineer</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Chinese_inventions" title="List of Chinese inventions">inventor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economist" title="Economist">economist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academies_(Shuyuan)" title="Academies (Shuyuan)">academy chancellor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finance_minister" title="Finance minister">finance minister</a>, governmental state inspector, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">philosopher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Art_criticism" title="Art criticism">art critic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_poetry" title="Chinese poetry">poet</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Music_of_China" title="Music of China">musician</a>. He was the head official for the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_astronomy" title="Chinese astronomy">Bureau of Astronomy</a> in the Song court, as well as an Assistant Minister of Imperial Hospitality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_4_part_2_33_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_4_part_2_33-2">[2]</a></sup> At court his political allegiance was to the Reformist faction known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Song_Dynasty#Partisans_and_factions,_reformers_and_conservatives" title="History of the Song Dynasty">New Policies Group</a>, headed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_China" title="Chancellor of China">Chancellor</a> <a href="/wiki/Wang_Anshi" title="Wang Anshi">Wang Anshi</a> (1021–1085).
</p><p>In his <i><a href="/wiki/Dream_Pool_Essays" title="Dream Pool Essays">Dream Pool Essays</a></i> or <i>Dream Torrent Essays</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Makeham2008_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Makeham2008-3">[3]</a></sup> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">夢溪筆談</span></span>; <i>Mengxi Bitan</i>) of 1088, Shen was the first to describe the magnetic needle <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">compass</a>, which would be used for navigation (first described in Europe by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Neckam" title="Alexander Neckam">Alexander Neckam</a> in 1187).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bowman_2000_599_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-bowman_2000_599-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mohn_2003_1_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-mohn_2003_1-5">[5]</a></sup> Shen discovered the concept of <a href="/wiki/True_north" title="True north">true north</a> in terms of <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_declination" title="Magnetic declination">magnetic declination</a> towards the <a href="/wiki/North_magnetic_pole" title="North magnetic pole">north pole</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mohn_2003_1_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-mohn_2003_1-5">[5]</a></sup> with experimentation of suspended magnetic needles and "the improved <a href="/wiki/Meridian_(astronomy)" title="Meridian (astronomy)">meridian</a> determined by Shen's [astronomical] measurement of the distance between the <a href="/wiki/Pole_star" title="Pole star">pole star</a> and true north".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_22_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_22-6">[6]</a></sup> This was the decisive step in human history to make compasses more useful for navigation, and may have been a concept unknown in Europe <a href="/wiki/Voyages_of_Christopher_Columbus" title="Voyages of Christopher Columbus">for another four hundred years</a> (evidence of German sundials made circa 1450 show markings similar to Chinese geomancers' compasses in regard to declination).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-embree_1997_843_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-embree_1997_843-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alongside his colleague <a href="/wiki/Wei_Pu" title="Wei Pu">Wei Pu</a>, Shen planned to map the orbital paths of the Moon and the planets in an intensive five-year project involving daily observations, yet this was thwarted by political opponents at court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_18_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_18-8">[8]</a></sup> To aid his work in astronomy, Shen Kuo made improved designs of the <a href="/wiki/Armillary_sphere" title="Armillary sphere">armillary sphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gnomon" title="Gnomon">gnomon</a>, sighting tube, and invented a new type of inflow <a href="/wiki/Water_clock" title="Water clock">water clock</a>. Shen Kuo devised a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geological" title="Geological">geological</a> hypothesis for land formation (<a href="/wiki/Geomorphology" title="Geomorphology">geomorphology</a>), based upon findings of inland <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_(ocean)" title="Marine (ocean)">marine</a> <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossils</a>, knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">soil erosion</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Deposition_(geology)" title="Deposition (geology)">deposition</a> of <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">silt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_23_24_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_23_24-9">[9]</a></sup> He also proposed a hypothesis of gradual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_change_(general_concept)" title="Climate change (general concept)">climate change</a>, after observing ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petrified" title="Petrified">petrified</a> <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">bamboos</a> that were preserved underground in a dry northern habitat that would not support bamboo growth in his time. He was the first literary figure in China to mention the use of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drydock" title="Drydock">drydock</a> to repair boats suspended out of water, and also wrote of the effectiveness of the relatively new invention of the canal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pound_lock" title="Pound lock">pound lock</a>. Although not the first to invent <a href="/wiki/Camera_obscura" title="Camera obscura">camera obscura</a>, Shen noted the relation of the <a href="/wiki/Focus_(optics)" title="Focus (optics)">focal point</a> of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concave_mirror" title="Concave mirror">concave mirror</a> and that of the pinhole. Shen wrote extensively about <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">movable type</a> <a href="/wiki/Printing" title="Printing">printing</a> invented by <a href="/wiki/Bi_Sheng" title="Bi Sheng">Bi Sheng</a> (990–1051), and because of his written works the legacy of Bi Sheng and the modern understanding of the earliest movable type has been handed down to later generations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bowman_2000_105_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-bowman_2000_105-10">[10]</a></sup> Following an old tradition in China, Shen created a <a href="/wiki/Raised-relief_map" title="Raised-relief map">raised-relief map</a> while inspecting borderlands. His description of an ancient crossbow mechanism which he himself unearthed proved to be a <a href="/wiki/Jacob%27s_staff" title="Jacob's staff">Jacob's staff</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Surveying" title="Surveying">surveying</a> tool which wasn't known in Europe until described by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Levi_ben_Gerson" title="Levi ben Gerson">Levi ben Gerson</a> in 1321.
</p><p>Shen Kuo wrote several other books besides the <i>Dream Pool Essays</i>, yet much of the writing in his other books has not survived. Some of Shen's poetry was preserved in posthumous written works. Although much of his focus was on technical and scientific issues, he had an interest in <a href="/wiki/Divination" title="Divination">divination</a> and the supernatural, the latter including his vivid description of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unidentified_flying_objects" title="Unidentified flying objects">unidentified flying objects</a> from eyewitness testimony. He also wrote commentary on ancient <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Daoist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucian</a> texts.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_and_youth">Birth and youth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Birth and youth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pen_ts%27ao,_woodblock_book_1249-ce.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1640" data-file-width="2286" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Pen_ts%27ao%2C_woodblock_book_1249-ce.png/220px-Pen_ts%27ao%2C_woodblock_book_1249-ce.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Pen_ts%27ao%2C_woodblock_book_1249-ce.png/330px-Pen_ts%27ao%2C_woodblock_book_1249-ce.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Pen_ts%27ao%2C_woodblock_book_1249-ce.png/440px-Pen_ts%27ao%2C_woodblock_book_1249-ce.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pen_ts%27ao,_woodblock_book_1249-ce.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i>Bencao</i> on <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">traditional Chinese medicine</a>; <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">printed with woodblock printing press</a> in 1249; Shen grew ill often as a child, and so developed an interest in medicinal cures.</div></div></div>
<p>Shen Kuo was born in Qiantang (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Hangzhou" title="Hangzhou">Hangzhou</a>) in the year 1031. His father Shen Zhou (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">沈周</span></span>; 978–1052) was a somewhat lower-class <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gentry_(China)" title="Gentry (China)">gentry</a> figure serving in official posts on the provincial level; his mother was from a family of equal status in <a href="/wiki/Suzhou" title="Suzhou">Suzhou</a>, with her maiden name being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X%C7%94_(surname)" title="Xǔ (surname)">Xu</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">許</span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_1_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_1-11">[11]</a></sup> Shen Kuo received his initial childhood education from his mother, which was a common practice in China during this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_1_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_1-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[a]</a></sup> She was very educated herself, teaching Kuo and his brother Pi (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">披</span></span>) the military doctrines of her own elder brother Xu Dong (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">許洞</span></span>; 975–1016).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_1_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_1-11">[11]</a></sup> Since Shen was unable to boast of a prominent familial clan history like many of his elite peers born in the north, he was forced to rely on his wit and stern determination to achieve in his studies, subsequently passing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_examinations" title="Imperial examinations">imperial examinations</a> and enter the challenging and sophisticated life of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scholar-bureaucrat" title="Scholar-bureaucrat">exam-drafted state bureaucrat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_1_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_1-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>From about 1040 AD, Shen's family moved around <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a> province and finally to the international seaport at <a href="/wiki/Xiamen" title="Xiamen">Xiamen</a>, where Shen's father accepted minor provincial posts in each new location.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_5_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_5-13">[12]</a></sup> Shen Zhou also served several years in the prestigious capital <a href="/wiki/Judiciary" title="Judiciary">judiciary</a>, the equivalent of a national supreme court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_1_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_1-11">[11]</a></sup> Shen Kuo took notice of the various towns and rural features of China as his family traveled, while he became interested during his youth in the diverse <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">topography</a> of the land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_5_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_5-13">[12]</a></sup> He also observed the intriguing aspects of his father's engagement in administrative governance and the managerial problems involved; these experiences had a deep impact on him as he later became a government official.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_5_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_5-13">[12]</a></sup> Since he often became ill as a child, Shen Kuo also developed a natural curiosity about medicine and pharmaceutics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_5_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_5-13">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shen Zhou died in the late winter of 1051 (or early 1052), when his son Shen Kuo was 21 years old. Shen Kuo grieved for his father, and following <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucian</a> ethics, remained inactive in a state of mourning for three years until 1054 (or early 1055).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_6_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_6-14">[13]</a></sup> As of 1054, Shen began serving in minor local governmental posts. However, his natural abilities to plan, organize, and design were proven early in life; one example is his design and supervision of the hydraulic drainage of an <a href="/wiki/Levee" title="Levee">embankment</a> system, <a href="/wiki/Land_reclamation" title="Land reclamation">which converted</a> some one hundred thousand <a href="/wiki/Acre" title="Acre">acres</a> (400 km<sup>2</sup>) of <a href="/wiki/Swamp" title="Swamp">swampland</a> into prime <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farmland_(farming)" title="Farmland (farming)">farmland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_6_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_6-14">[13]</a></sup> Shen Kuo noted that the success of the <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">silt</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertilization" title="Fertilization">fertilization</a> method relied upon the effective operation of <a href="/wiki/Sluice" title="Sluice">sluice</a> gates of irrigation <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">canals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_4_part_3_230_231_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_4_part_3_230_231-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_career">Official career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Official career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shenzong_of_Song.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2575" data-file-width="1675" decoding="async" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Shenzong_of_Song.jpg/170px-Shenzong_of_Song.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Shenzong_of_Song.jpg/255px-Shenzong_of_Song.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Shenzong_of_Song.jpg/340px-Shenzong_of_Song.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shenzong_of_Song.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shen's confidant, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Shenzong_of_Song" title="Emperor Shenzong of Song">Emperor Shenzong of Song</a> (r. 1067–1085), a Song era portrait painting.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1063 Shen Kuo successfully passed the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_examination" title="Imperial examination">Imperial examinations</a>, the difficult national-level standard test that every high official was required to pass in order to enter the governmental system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_6_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_6-14">[13]</a></sup> He not only passed the exam however, but was placed into the higher category of the best and brightest students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_6_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_6-14">[13]</a></sup> While serving at <a href="/wiki/Yangzhou" title="Yangzhou">Yangzhou</a>, Shen's brilliance and dutiful character caught the attention of Zhang Chu (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">張蒭</span></span>; 1015–1080), the Fiscal Intendant of the region. Shen made a lasting impression upon Zhang, who recommended Shen for a court appointment in the financial administration of the central court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_6_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_6-14">[13]</a></sup> Shen would also eventually marry Zhang's daughter, who became his second wife.
</p><p>In his career as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scholar-bureaucrats" title="Scholar-bureaucrats">scholar-official</a> for the central government, Shen Kuo was also an ambassador to the <a href="/wiki/Western_Xia" title="Western Xia">Western Xia</a> Dynasty and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liao_Dynasty" title="Liao Dynasty">Liao Dynasty</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-steinhardt_1997_316_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-steinhardt_1997_316-16">[15]</a></sup> a military commander, a director of hydraulic works, and the leading chancellor of the <a href="/wiki/Hanlin_Academy" title="Hanlin Academy">Hanlin Academy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_1_135_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_1_135-17">[16]</a></sup> By 1072, Shen was appointed as the head official of the Bureau of Astronomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_6_14-5"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_6-14">[13]</a></sup> With his leadership position in the bureau, Shen was responsible for projects in improving calendrical science,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bowman_2000_105_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-bowman_2000_105-10">[10]</a></sup> and proposed many reforms to the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calendar" title="Chinese calendar">Chinese calendar</a> alongside the work of his colleague <a href="/wiki/Wei_Pu" title="Wei Pu">Wei Pu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_18_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_18-8">[8]</a></sup> With his impressive skills and aptitude for matters of economy and finance, Shen was appointed as the Finance Commissioner at the central court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_9_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_9-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>As written by Li Zhiyi, a man married to Hu Wenrou (granddaughter of Hu Su, a famous minister of the Song Dynasty), Shen Kuo was Li's mentor while Shen served as an official.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tao_2004_19_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-tao_2004_19-19">[18]</a></sup> According to Li's epitaph for his wife, Shen would sometimes relay questions via Li to Hu when he needed clarification for his mathematical work, as Hu Wenrou was esteemed by Shen as a remarkable female mathematician.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tao_2004_19_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-tao_2004_19-19">[18]</a></sup> Shen lamented: "If only she were a man, Wenrou would be my friend."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tao_2004_19_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-tao_2004_19-19">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>While employed by the central government, Shen Kuo was also sent out with others to inspect the granary system of the empire, investigating problems of illegal tax-collection, negligence, ineffective disaster relief, and inadequate water-conservancy projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hymes_schirokauer_1993_109_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-hymes_schirokauer_1993_109-20">[19]</a></sup> While Shen was appointed as the regional inspector of Zhejiang in 1073, the Emperor requested that Shen pay a visit to the famous poet <a href="/wiki/Su_Shi" title="Su Shi">Su Shi</a> (1037–1101), then an administrator in Hangzhou.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hartman_1990_22_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-hartman_1990_22-21">[20]</a></sup> Shen took advantage of this meeting to copy some of Su's poetry, which he presented to the Emperor indicating that it expressed "abusive and hateful" speech against the Song court; these poems were later politicized by Li Ding and Shu Dan in order to level a court case against Su. (The <a href="/wiki/Crow_Terrace_Poetry_Trial" title="Crow Terrace Poetry Trial">Crow Terrace Poetry Trial</a>, of 1079.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hartman_1990_22_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-hartman_1990_22-21">[20]</a></sup> With his demonstrations of loyalty and ability, Shen Kuo was awarded the honorary title of a State Foundation <a href="/wiki/Viscount" title="Viscount">Viscount</a> by <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Shenzong_of_Song" title="Emperor Shenzong of Song">Emperor Shenzong of Song</a> (r. 1067–1085), who placed a great amount of trust in Shen Kuo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_9_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_9-18">[17]</a></sup> He was even made 'companion to the heir apparent' (太子中允; 'Taizi zhongyun').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yao_2003_544_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-yao_2003_544-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wang_Anshi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2011" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Wang_Anshi.jpg/170px-Wang_Anshi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Wang_Anshi.jpg/255px-Wang_Anshi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Wang_Anshi.jpg/340px-Wang_Anshi.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wang_Anshi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait painting of <a href="/wiki/Wang_Anshi" title="Wang Anshi">Wang Anshi</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>At court Shen was a political favorite of the Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Wang_Anshi" title="Wang Anshi">Wang Anshi</a> (1021–1086), who was the leader of the political faction of Reformers, also known as the New Policies Group (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">新法</span></span>, Xin Fa).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_3_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_3-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[b]</a></sup> Shen Kuo had a previous history with Wang Anshi, since it was Wang who had composed the funerary <a href="/wiki/Epitaph" title="Epitaph">epitaph</a> for Shen's father, Zhou.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_7_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_7-24">[22]</a></sup> Shen Kuo soon impressed Wang Anshi with his skills and abilities as an administrator and government agent. In 1072, Shen was sent to supervise Wang's program of surveying the building of silt deposits in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Canal_of_China" title="Grand Canal of China">Bian Canal</a> outside the capital city. Using an original technique, Shen successfully dredged the canal and demonstrated the formidable value of the silt gathered as a <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_7_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_7-24">[22]</a></sup> He gained further reputation at court once he was dispatched as an envoy to the <a href="/wiki/Khitan_people" title="Khitan people">Khitan</a> Liao Dynasty in the summer of 1075.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_7_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_7-24">[22]</a></sup> The Khitans had made several aggressive negotiations of pushing their borders south, while manipulating several incompetent Song ambassadors who conceded to the Liao Kingdom's demands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_7_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_7-24">[22]</a></sup> In a brilliant display of diplomacy, Shen Kuo came to the camp of the Khitan monarch at Mt. Yongan (near modern <a href="/wiki/Pingquan" title="Pingquan">Pingquan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hebei" title="Hebei">Hebei</a>), armed with copies of previously archived diplomatic negotiations between the Song and Liao dynasties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_7_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_7-24">[22]</a></sup> Shen Kuo refuted <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Daozong_of_Liao" title="Emperor Daozong of Liao">Emperor Daozong's</a> bluffs point for point, while the Song reestablished their rightful border line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_7_24-5"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_7-24">[22]</a></sup> In regard to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L%C3%BD_Dynasty" title="Lý Dynasty">Lý Dynasty</a> of <a href="/wiki/%C4%90%E1%BA%A1i_Vi%E1%BB%87t" title="Đại Việt">Đại Việt</a> (in modern northern <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>), Shen demonstrated in his <i>Dream Pool Essays</i> that he was familiar with the key players (on the Vietnamese side) in the prelude to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Song_Dynasty#Relations_with_Lý_of_Vietnam_and_border_conflict" title="History of the Song Dynasty">Sino-Vietnamese War of 1075–1077</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup> With his reputable achievements, Shen became a trusted member of Wang Anshi's elite circle of eighteen unofficial core political loyalists to the New Policies Group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_7_24-6"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_7-24">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:China_11a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="560" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/China_11a.jpg/220px-China_11a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/China_11a.jpg/330px-China_11a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/China_11a.jpg/440px-China_11a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:China_11a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Boundaries of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Song_Dynasty" title="Northern Song Dynasty">Northern Song Dynasty</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liao_Dynasty" title="Liao Dynasty">Liao Dynasty</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Western_Xia" title="Western Xia">Western Xia</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Although much of Wang Anshi's reforms outlined in the <a href="/wiki/New_Policies_(Song_dynasty)" title="New Policies (Song dynasty)">New Policies</a> centered on state finance, land tax reform, and the Imperial examinations, there were also military concerns. This included policies of raising <a href="/wiki/Militia" title="Militia">militias</a> to lessen the expense of upholding a million soldiers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ebrey_walthall_palais_2006_164_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-ebrey_walthall_palais_2006_164-26">[24]</a></sup> putting government monopolies on <a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">saltpetre</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulphur" title="Sulphur">sulphur</a> production and distribution in 1076 (to ensure that <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a> solutions would not fall into the hands of enemies),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_5_part_7_126_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_5_part_7_126-27">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zhang_1986_489_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-zhang_1986_489-28">[26]</a></sup> and aggressive military policy towards Song's northern rivals of the Western Xia and Liao dynasties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III,_4-5_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III,_4-5-29">[27]</a></sup> A few years after Song Dynasty military forces had made victorious territorial gains against the <a href="/wiki/Tangut_people" title="Tangut people">Tanguts</a> of the Western Xia, in 1080 Shen Kuo was entrusted as a military officer in defense of Yanzhou (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Yan%27an" title="Yan'an">Yan'an</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">Shaanxi</a> province).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_8_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_8-30">[28]</a></sup> During the autumn months of 1081, Shen was successful in defending Song Dynasty territory while capturing several fortified towns of the Western Xia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_9_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_9-18">[17]</a></sup> The Emperor Shenzong of Song rewarded Shen with numerous titles for his merit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Song-Xia_wars" title="Song-Xia wars">in these battles</a>, and in the sixteen months of Shen's military campaign, he received 273 letters from the Emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_9_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_9-18">[17]</a></sup> However, Emperor Shenzong trusted an arrogant military officer who disobeyed the emperor and Shen's proposal for strategic fortifications, instead fortifying what Shen considered useless strategic locations. Furthermore, this officer expelled Shen from his commanding post at the main <a href="/wiki/Citadel" title="Citadel">citadel</a>, so as to deny him any glory in chance of victory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_9_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_9-18">[17]</a></sup> The result of this was nearly catastrophic, as the forces of the arrogant officer were decimated;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_9_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_9-18">[17]</a></sup> Xinzhong Yao states that the death toll was 60,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yao_2003_544_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-yao_2003_544-1">[1]</a></sup> Nonetheless, Shen was successful in defending his fortifications and the only possible Tangut invasion-route to Yanzhou.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_9_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_9-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impeachment_and_later_life">Impeachment and later life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Impeachment and later life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Liu_Songnian-Luohan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1772" data-file-width="851" decoding="async" height="354" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Liu_Songnian-Luohan.jpg/170px-Liu_Songnian-Luohan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Liu_Songnian-Luohan.jpg/255px-Liu_Songnian-Luohan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Liu_Songnian-Luohan.jpg/340px-Liu_Songnian-Luohan.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Liu_Songnian-Luohan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Painting of a Buddhist <a href="/wiki/Arhat" title="Arhat">luohan</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Liu_Songnian" title="Liu Songnian">Liu Songnian</a>, painted in 1207; Shen Kuo not only listed literati painting as one of his cherished pastimes, but also <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_meditation" title="Buddhist meditation">Buddhist meditation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lian_2001_20_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-lian_2001_20-31">[29]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The new Chancellor Cai Que (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">蔡確</span></span>; 1036–1093) held Shen responsible for the disaster and loss of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_9_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_9-18">[17]</a></sup> Along with abandoning the territory which Shen Kuo had fought for, Cai ousted Shen from his seat of office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_9_18-8"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_9-18">[17]</a></sup> Shen's life was now forever changed, as he lost his once reputable career in state governance and the military.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_9_18-9"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_9-18">[17]</a></sup> Shen was then put under <a href="/wiki/Probation" title="Probation">probation</a> in a fixed residence for the next six years. However, as he was isolated from governance, he decided to pick up the <a href="/wiki/Ink_brush" title="Ink brush">ink brush</a> and dedicate himself to intensive scholarly studies. After completing two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geographical" title="Geographical">geographical</a> <a href="/wiki/Atlas" title="Atlas">atlases</a> for a state-sponsored program, Shen was rewarded by having his sentence of probation lifted, allowing him to live in a place of his choice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_9_18-10"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_9-18">[17]</a></sup> Shen was also pardoned by the court for any previous faults or crimes that were claimed against him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_9_18-11"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_9-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his more idle years removed from court affairs, Shen Kuo enjoyed pastimes of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gentry_(China)" title="Gentry (China)">the Chinese gentry and literati</a> that would indicate his intellectual level and cultural taste to others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lian_2001_24_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-lian_2001_24-32">[30]</a></sup> As described in his <i>Dream Pool Essays</i>, Shen Kuo enjoyed the company of the "nine guests" (九客, <i>jiuke</i>), a figure of speech for the <a href="/wiki/Guqin" title="Guqin">Chinese zither</a>, the older 17x17 line variant of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weiqi" title="Weiqi">weiqi</a> (known today as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weiqi" title="Weiqi">go</a>), <a href="/wiki/Zen" title="Zen">Zen</a> <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_meditation" title="Buddhist meditation">Buddhist meditation</a>, ink (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_calligraphy" title="Chinese calligraphy">calligraphy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_painting" title="Chinese painting">painting</a>), <a href="/wiki/Chinese_tea" title="Chinese tea">tea drinking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_poetry" title="Chinese poetry">chanting poetry</a>, conversation, and <a href="/wiki/Wine_in_China" title="Wine in China">drinking wine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lian_2001_20_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-lian_2001_20-31">[29]</a></sup> These nine activities were an extension to the older so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four_Arts_of_the_Chinese_Scholar" title="Four Arts of the Chinese Scholar">Four Arts of the Chinese Scholar</a>.
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Yu_(author)" title="Zhu Yu (author)">Zhu Yu</a>'s book <i>Pingzhou Table Talks</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">萍洲可談</span></span>; <i>Pingzhou Ketan</i>) of 1119, Shen Kuo had two marriages; the second wife was the daughter of Zhang Chu (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">張蒭</span></span>), who came from <a href="/wiki/Huainan" title="Huainan">Huainan</a>. Lady Zhang was said to be overbearing and fierce, often abusive to Shen Kuo, even attempting at one time to pull off his beard. Shen Kuo's children were often upset over this, and prostrated themselves to Lady Zhang to quit this behavior. Despite this, Lady Zhang went as far as to drive out Shen Kuo's son from his first marriage, expelling him from the household. However, after Lady Zhang died, Shen Kuo fell into a deep depression and even attempted to jump into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River" title="Yangtze River">Yangtze River</a> to drown himself. Although this suicide attempt failed, he would die a year later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hongen_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-hongen-33">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1070s, Shen had purchased a lavish garden estate on the outskirts of modern-day <a href="/wiki/Zhenjiang" title="Zhenjiang">Zhenjiang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a> province, a place of great beauty which he named "Dream Brook" ("Mengxi") after he visited it for the first time in 1086.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_9_18-12"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_9-18">[17]</a></sup> Shen Kuo permanently moved to the Dream Brook Estate in 1088, and in that same year he completed his life's written work of the <i><a href="/wiki/Dream_Pool_Essays" title="Dream Pool Essays">Dream Pool Essays</a></i>, naming the book after his garden-estate property. It was there that Shen Kuo spent the last several years of his life in leisure, isolation, and illness, until his death in 1095.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_9_18-13"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_9-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scholarly_achievements">Scholarly achievements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Scholarly achievements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Science and technology of the Song dynasty">Science and technology of the Song dynasty</a></div>
<p>Shen Kuo wrote extensively on a wide range of different subjects. His written work included two geographical <a href="/wiki/Atlas" title="Atlas">atlases</a>, a treatise on <a href="/wiki/Music" title="Music">music</a> with mathematical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harmonics" title="Harmonics">harmonics</a>, governmental administration, mathematical astronomy, astronomical instruments, martial <a href="/wiki/Military_strategy" title="Military strategy">defensive tactics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fortification" title="Fortification">fortifications</a>, <a href="/wiki/Painting" title="Painting">painting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tea" title="Tea">tea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>, and much <a href="/wiki/Chinese_poetry" title="Chinese poetry">poetry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_10_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_10-34">[32]</a></sup> His scientific writings have been praised by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinologist" title="Sinologist">sinologists</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Needham" title="Joseph Needham">Joseph Needham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nathan_Sivin" title="Nathan Sivin">Nathan Sivin</a>, and he has been compared by Sivin to polymaths such as his Song dynasty Chinese contemporary <a href="/wiki/Su_Song" title="Su Song">Su Song</a>, as well as to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gottfried_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Leibniz">Gottfried Leibniz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Lomonosov" title="Mikhail Lomonosov">Mikhail Lomonosov</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_11_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_11-35">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Raised-relief_map">Raised-relief map</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Raised-relief map">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FFM-HanWeingef%C3%A4%C3%9F.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2394" data-file-width="1988" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/FFM-HanWeingef%C3%A4%C3%9F.JPG/170px-FFM-HanWeingef%C3%A4%C3%9F.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/FFM-HanWeingef%C3%A4%C3%9F.JPG/255px-FFM-HanWeingef%C3%A4%C3%9F.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/FFM-HanWeingef%C3%A4%C3%9F.JPG/340px-FFM-HanWeingef%C3%A4%C3%9F.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FFM-HanWeingef%C3%A4%C3%9F.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han Dynasty</a> incense burner, showing artificial mountains as a lid decoration, which may have influenced the invention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_580_581_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_580_581-36">[34]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Raised-relief_map#History" title="Raised-relief map">Raised-relief map § History</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_cartography#China" title="History of cartography">History of cartography § China</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_of_the_Han_Dynasty#Cartography" title="Science and technology of the Han Dynasty">Science and technology of the Han Dynasty § Cartography</a></div>
<p>Joseph Needham suggests that certain pottery vessels of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han Dynasty</a> (202 BC – 220 AD) showing artificial mountains as lid decorations may have influenced the development of the <a href="/wiki/Raised-relief_map" title="Raised-relief map">raised-relief map</a> in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_580_581_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_580_581-36">[34]</a></sup> The Han Dynasty general <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ma_Yuan_(Han_Dynasty)" title="Ma Yuan (Han Dynasty)">Ma Yuan</a> (14 BC – 49 AD) is recorded as having made a raised-relief map of valleys and mountains in a rice-constructed model of 32 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crespigny_2007_659_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-crespigny_2007_659-37">[35]</a></sup> Shen Kuo's largest atlas included twenty three maps of China and foreign regions that were drawn at a uniform scale of 1:900,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_22_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_22-6">[6]</a></sup> Shen also created a raised-relief map using sawdust, wood, beeswax, and wheat paste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_22_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_22-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_579_580_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_579_580-38">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Xi" title="Zhu Xi">Zhu Xi</a> (1130–1200) was inspired by the raised-relief map of Huang Shang and so made his own portable map made of wood and clay which could be folded up from eight hinged pieces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pharmacology">Pharmacology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Pharmacology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">Traditional Chinese medicine</a></div>
<p>For <a href="/wiki/Pharmacology" title="Pharmacology">pharmacology</a>, Shen wrote of the difficulties of adequate <a href="/wiki/Medical_diagnosis" title="Medical diagnosis">diagnosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pharmacotherapy" title="Pharmacotherapy">therapy</a>, as well as the proper selection, preparation, and administration of drugs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_29_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_29-40">[38]</a></sup> He held great concern for detail and <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philological</a> accuracy in identification, use and cultivation of different types of medicinal herbs, such as in which months medicinal plants should be gathered, their exact ripening times, which parts should be used for therapy; for domesticated herbs he wrote about planting times, fertilization, and other matters of <a href="/wiki/Horticulture" title="Horticulture">horticulture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_30_31_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_30_31-41">[39]</a></sup> In the realms of <a href="/wiki/Botany" title="Botany">botany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mineralogy" title="Mineralogy">mineralogy</a>, Shen Kuo documented and systematically described hundreds of different plants, agricultural crops, rare vegetation, animals, and <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a> found in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_6_part_1_475_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_6_part_1_475-42">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_6_part_1_499_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_6_part_1_499-43">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_6_part_1_501_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_6_part_1_501-44">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_30_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_30-45">[43]</a></sup> For example, Shen noted that the mineral <a href="/wiki/Orpiment" title="Orpiment">orpiment</a> was used to quickly erase writing errors on paper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_engineering">Civil engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Civil engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Canal_lock.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Canal_lock.svg/170px-Canal_lock.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Canal_lock.svg/255px-Canal_lock.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Canal_lock.svg/340px-Canal_lock.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Canal_lock.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A side view of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pound_lock" title="Pound lock">pound lock</a> for canals, invented in China in the 10th century and described by Shen.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yingzao_Fashi_5_desmear.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="815" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Yingzao_Fashi_5_desmear.JPG/170px-Yingzao_Fashi_5_desmear.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Yingzao_Fashi_5_desmear.JPG/255px-Yingzao_Fashi_5_desmear.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Yingzao_Fashi_5_desmear.JPG/340px-Yingzao_Fashi_5_desmear.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yingzao_Fashi_5_desmear.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Five bracket arm bases and two cantilever arms, from the <i><a href="/wiki/Yingzao_Fashi" title="Yingzao Fashi">Yingzao Fashi</a></i> of 1103.</div></div></div>
<p>The writing of Shen Kuo is the only source for the date when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drydock" title="Drydock">drydock</a> was first used in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_4_part_3_660_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_4_part_3_660-47">[45]</a></sup> Shen Kuo wrote that during the Xi-Ning reign (1068–1077), the court official Huang Huaixin devised a plan for repairing 60 m (200 ft) long <a href="/wiki/Palace" title="Palace">palatial</a> boats that were a century old; essentially, Huang Huaixin devised the first Chinese drydock for suspending boats out of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_4_part_3_660_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_4_part_3_660-47">[45]</a></sup> These boats were then placed in a roof-covered dock warehouse to protect them from weathering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_4_part_3_660_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_4_part_3_660-47">[45]</a></sup> Shen  also wrote about the effectiveness of the new invention (i.e. by the 10th century engineer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Technology_of_the_Song_Dynasty" title="Technology of the Song Dynasty">Qiao Weiyo</a>) of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pound_lock" title="Pound lock">pound lock</a> to replace the old <a href="/wiki/Flash_lock" title="Flash lock">flash lock</a> design used in canals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_4_part_3_352_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_4_part_3_352-48">[46]</a></sup> He wrote that it saved the work of five hundred annual labors, annual costs of up to 1,250,000 strings of cash, and increased the size limit of boats accommodated from  21 <a href="/wiki/Ton" title="Ton">tons</a>/21000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kg" title="Kg">kg</a> to 113 <a href="/wiki/Ton" title="Ton">tons</a>/115000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kg" title="Kg">kg</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_4_part_3_352_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_4_part_3_352-48">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>If it were not for Shen Kuo's analysis and quoting in his <i><a href="/wiki/Dream_Pool_Essays" title="Dream Pool Essays">Dream Pool Essays</a></i> of the writings of the <a href="/wiki/Architect" title="Architect">architect</a> <a href="/wiki/Yu_Hao" title="Yu Hao">Yu Hao</a> (<a href="/wiki/Floruit" title="Floruit">fl.</a> 970), the latter's work would have been lost to history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_4_part_3_141_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_4_part_3_141-49">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[c]</a></sup> Yu designed a famous wooden <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_pagoda" title="Chinese pagoda">pagoda</a> that burned down in 1044 and was replaced in 1049 by a brick pagoda (the '<a href="/wiki/Iron_Pagoda" title="Iron Pagoda">Iron Pagoda</a>') of similar height, but not of his design. From Shen's quotation—or perhaps Shen's own paraphrasing of Yu Hao's <i>Timberwork Manual</i> (木經; <i>Mujing</i>)—shows that already in the 10th century there was a graded system of building unit proportions, a system which Shen states had become more precise in his time but stating no one could possibly reproduce such a sound work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ruitenbeek_1996_26_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-ruitenbeek_1996_26-51">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chung_2004_19_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-chung_2004_19-52">[49]</a></sup> However, he did not anticipate the more complex and matured system of unit proportions embodied in the extensive written work by scholar-official Li Jie (1065–1110), the <i><a href="/wiki/Yingzao_Fashi" title="Yingzao Fashi">Treatise on Architectural Methods</a></i> (營造法式; <i>Yingzao Fashi</i>) of 1103.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chung_2004_19_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-chung_2004_19-52">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[50]</a></sup> Klaas Ruitenbeek states that the version of the <i>Timberwork Manual</i> quoted by Shen is most likely Shen's summarization of Yu's work or a corrupted passage of the original by Yu Hao, as Shen writes: "According to some, the work was written by Yu Hao."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ruitenbeek_1996_26_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-ruitenbeek_1996_26-51">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy">Anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Chinese had long taken an interest in examining the human body. For example, in 16 AD, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xin_Dynasty" title="Xin Dynasty">Xin Dynasty</a> usurper <a href="/wiki/Wang_Mang" title="Wang Mang">Wang Mang</a> called for the dissection of an executed man, to examine his arteries and viscera in order to discover cures for illnesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[51]</a></sup> Shen also took interest in human <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomy</a>, dispelling the long-held Chinese theory that the throat contained three valves, writing, "When liquid and solid are imbibed together, how can it be that in one's mouth they sort themselves into two throat channels?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_30_31_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_30_31-41">[39]</a></sup> Shen maintained that the <a href="/wiki/Larynx" title="Larynx">larynx</a> was the beginning of a system that distributed vital <i><a href="/wiki/Qi" title="Qi">qi</a></i> from the air throughout the body, and that the <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">esophagus</a> was a simple tube that dropped food into the stomach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_31_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_31-55">[52]</a></sup> Following Shen's reasoning and correcting the findings of the <a href="/wiki/Dissection" title="Dissection">dissection</a> of executed bandits in 1045, an early 12th-century Chinese account of a bodily dissection finally supported Shen's belief in two throat valves, not three.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_30_31_footnote_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_30_31_footnote-56">[53]</a></sup>  Also, the later Song Dynasty judge and early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forensic" title="Forensic">forensic</a> expert <a href="/wiki/Song_Ci" title="Song Ci">Song Ci</a> (1186–1249) would promote the use of <a href="/wiki/Autopsy" title="Autopsy">autopsy</a> in order to solve <a href="/wiki/Homicide" title="Homicide">homicide</a> cases, as written in his <i><a href="/wiki/Collected_Cases_of_Injustice_Rectified" title="Collected Cases of Injustice Rectified">Collected Cases of Injustice Rectified</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sung_1981_12,_19,_20,_72_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sung_1981_12,_19,_20,_72-57">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematics">Mathematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Mathematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_mathematics" title="Chinese mathematics">Chinese mathematics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yanghui_triangle.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1095" data-file-width="704" decoding="async" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Yanghui_triangle.gif/170px-Yanghui_triangle.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Yanghui_triangle.gif/255px-Yanghui_triangle.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Yanghui_triangle.gif/340px-Yanghui_triangle.gif 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yanghui_triangle.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Yang_Hui" title="Yang Hui">Yang Hui</a> triangle (<a href="/wiki/Pascal%27s_triangle" title="Pascal's triangle">Pascal's triangle</a>) using <a href="/wiki/Counting_rods" title="Counting rods">rod numerals</a>, from a book by mathematician <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Shijie" title="Zhu Shijie">Zhu Shijie</a>, 1303</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Camera_obscura.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="580" data-file-width="363" decoding="async" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Camera_obscura.jpg/170px-Camera_obscura.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Camera_obscura.jpg/255px-Camera_obscura.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Camera_obscura.jpg/340px-Camera_obscura.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Camera_obscura.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 18th-century diagram of <a href="/wiki/Camera_obscura" title="Camera obscura">camera obscura</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the broad field of <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, Shen Kuo mastered many practical mathematical problems, including many complex formulas for <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">geometry</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_39_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_39-58">[55]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Circle_packing" title="Circle packing">circle packing</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_145_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_145-59">[56]</a></sup> and chords and arcs problems employing <a href="/wiki/Trigonometry" title="Trigonometry">trigonometry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_109_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_109-60">[57]</a></sup> Shen addressed problems of writing out very large numbers, as large as (10<sup>4</sup>)<sup>43</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-katz_2007_308_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-katz_2007_308-61">[58]</a></sup> Shen's "technique of small increments" laid the foundation in Chinese mathematics for packing problems involving equal difference series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-katz_2007_308_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-katz_2007_308-61">[58]</a></sup> Sal Restivo writes that Shen used summation of higher series to ascertain the number of kegs which could be piled in layers in a space shaped like the <a href="/wiki/Frustum" title="Frustum">frustum</a> of a rectangular pyramid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-restivo_1992_32_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-restivo_1992_32-62">[59]</a></sup> In his formula "technique of intersecting circles", he created an approximation of the arc of a circle <i>s</i> given the diameter <i>d</i>, <a href="/wiki/Sagitta_(geometry)" title="Sagitta (geometry)">sagitta</a> <i>v</i>, and length of the chord <i>c</i> subtending the arc, the length of which he approximated as <i>s</i> = <i>c</i> + 2v<sup>2</sup>/d.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-katz_2007_308_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-katz_2007_308-61">[58]</a></sup> Restivo writes that Shen's work in the lengths of arcs of circles provided the basis for <a href="/wiki/Spherical_trigonometry" title="Spherical trigonometry">spherical trigonometry</a> developed in the 13th century by <a href="/wiki/Guo_Shoujing" title="Guo Shoujing">Guo Shoujing</a> (1231–1316).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-restivo_1992_32_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-restivo_1992_32-62">[59]</a></sup> He also simplified the <a href="/wiki/Counting_rods" title="Counting rods">counting rods</a> technique by outlining short cuts in algorithm procedures used on the counting board, an idea expanded on by the mathematician <a href="/wiki/Yang_Hui" title="Yang Hui">Yang Hui</a> (1238–1298).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-katz_2007_308_309_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-katz_2007_308_309-63">[60]</a></sup> Victor J. Katz asserts that Shen's method of "dividing by 9, increase by 1; dividing by 8, increase by 2," was a direct forerunner to the rhyme scheme method of repeated addition "9, 1, bottom add 1; 9, 2, bottom add 2".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-katz_2007_309_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-katz_2007_309-64">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shen wrote extensively about what he had learned while working for the state treasury, including mathematical problems posed by computing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Land_tax" title="Land tax">land tax</a>, estimating requirements, <a href="/wiki/Currency" title="Currency">currency</a> issues, <a href="/wiki/Metrology" title="Metrology">metrology</a>, and so forth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_12_14_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_12_14-65">[62]</a></sup> Shen once computed the amount of <a href="/wiki/Terrain" title="Terrain">terrain</a> space required for battle formations in <a href="/wiki/Military_strategy" title="Military strategy">military strategy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_14_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_14-66">[63]</a></sup> and also computed the longest possible military campaign given the limits of human carriers who would bring their own food and food for other soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ebrey_2006_162_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-ebrey_2006_162-67">[64]</a></sup>  Shen wrote about the earlier <a href="/wiki/Yi_Xing" title="Yi Xing">Yi Xing</a> (672–717), a Buddhist monk who applied an early <a href="/wiki/Escapement" title="Escapement">escapement</a> mechanism to a water-powered <a href="/wiki/Celestial_globe" title="Celestial globe">celestial globe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[65]</a></sup> By using mathematical <a href="/wiki/Permutation" title="Permutation">permutations</a>, Shen described Yi Xing's calculation of possible positions on a <a href="/wiki/Go_(game)" title="Go (game)"><i>go</i> board game</a>. Shen calculated the total number for this using up to five rows and twenty five game pieces, which yielded the number 847,288,609,443.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_15_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_15-69">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_139_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_139-70">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Optics">Optics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Optics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Shen Kuo experimented with the <a href="/wiki/Pinhole_camera" title="Pinhole camera">pinhole camera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burning_glass" title="Burning glass">burning mirror</a> as the ancient Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohist" title="Mohist">Mohists</a> had done in the 4th century BC, as <a href="/wiki/Mozi" title="Mozi">Mozi</a> of China's <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a> was perhaps the first to describe the concept of <a href="/wiki/Camera_obscura" title="Camera obscura">camera obscura</a>, if not his Greek contemporary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Problems_(Aristotle)" title="Problems (Aristotle)">Aristotle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraqi</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> scientist <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Haytham" title="Ibn al-Haytham">Ibn al-Haytham</a> (965–1039) further experimented with camera obscura and was the first to attribute <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geometrical" title="Geometrical">geometrical</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantitative_property" title="Quantitative property">quantitative</a> properties to it, but Shen was first to note the relationship of the three separate radiation phenomena: the focal point, burning point, and pinhole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_4_part_1_98–99_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_4_part_1_98–99-72">[69]</a></sup> Using a fitting metaphor, Shen compared optical image inversion to an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oarlock" title="Oarlock">oarlock</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waisted_drum" title="Waisted drum">waisted drum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_34_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_34-73">[70]</a></sup> Along with <a href="/wiki/Focus_(optics)" title="Focus (optics)">focal points</a>, he also noted that the image in a <a href="/wiki/Curved_mirror" title="Curved mirror">concave mirror</a> is inverted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sarkar_salazar-palma_sengupta_2006_21_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-sarkar_salazar-palma_sengupta_2006_21-74">[71]</a></sup> Shen, who never asserted that he was the first to experiment with camera obscura, hints in his writing that camera obscura was dealt with in the <i><a href="/wiki/Miscellaneous_Morsels_from_Youyang" title="Miscellaneous Morsels from Youyang">Miscellaneous Morsels from Youyang</a></i> written by <a href="/wiki/Duan_Chengshi" title="Duan Chengshi">Duan Chengshi</a> (d. 863) during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" title="Tang Dynasty">Tang Dynasty</a> (618–907), in regard to the inverted image of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_pagoda" title="Chinese pagoda">Chinese pagoda</a> by a seashore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_4_part_1_98_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_4_part_1_98-75">[72]</a></sup> Chinese authors from the 12th to 17th centuries would discuss the optical observations made by Shen Kuo but not advance them further, while <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a> (1452–1519) would be the first in Europe to make a similar observation about the focal point and pinhole in camera obscura.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_4_part_1_98–99_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_4_part_1_98–99-72">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_needle_compass">Magnetic needle compass</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Magnetic needle compass">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Chinese_inventions" title="List of Chinese inventions">List of Chinese inventions</a></div>
<p>Since the time of the engineer and inventor <a href="/wiki/Ma_Jun_(mechanical_engineer)" title="Ma Jun (mechanical engineer)">Ma Jun</a> (c. 200–265), the Chinese had used the <a href="/wiki/South-pointing_chariot" title="South-pointing chariot">south-pointing chariot</a>, which did not employ magnetism, as a compass. In 1044 the <i><a href="/wiki/Wujing_Zongyao" title="Wujing Zongyao">Collection of the Most Important Military Techniques</a></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">武經總要</span></span>; <i>Wujing Zongyao</i>) recorded that fish-shaped objects cut from sheet iron, magnetized by <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/en:thermoremanence" title="wiktionary:en:thermoremanence">thermoremanence</a> (essentially, heating that produced weak magnetic force), and placed in a water-filled bowl enclosed by a box were used for directional pathfinding alongside the south-pointing chariot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_21_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_21-76">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_4_part_1_252_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_4_part_1_252-77">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Model_Si_Nan_of_Han_Dynasty.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Model_Si_Nan_of_Han_Dynasty.jpg/220px-Model_Si_Nan_of_Han_Dynasty.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Model_Si_Nan_of_Han_Dynasty.jpg/330px-Model_Si_Nan_of_Han_Dynasty.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Model_Si_Nan_of_Han_Dynasty.jpg/440px-Model_Si_Nan_of_Han_Dynasty.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Model_Si_Nan_of_Han_Dynasty.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han Dynasty</a> (202 BC–220 AD) ladle-and-basin <a href="/wiki/Lodestone" title="Lodestone">lodestone</a> south-pointing <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">compass</a>, used by ancient Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geomancer" title="Geomancer">geomancers</a>, but not for navigation.</div></div></div>
<p>However, it was not until the time of Shen Kuo that the earliest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic" title="Magnetic">magnetic</a> <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">compasses</a> would be used for <a href="/wiki/Navigation" title="Navigation">navigation</a>. In his written work, Shen Kuo made the first known explicit reference to the magnetic compass-needle and the concept of <a href="/wiki/True_north" title="True north">true north</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_1_135_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_1_135-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_4_part_1_249_250_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_4_part_1_249_250-78">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hsu_1988_102_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-hsu_1988_102-79">[76]</a></sup> He wrote that steel needles were magnetized once they were rubbed with <a href="/wiki/Lodestone" title="Lodestone">lodestone</a>, and that they were put in floating position or in mountings; he described the suspended compass as the best form to be used, and noted that the magnetic needle of compasses pointed either south or north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_21_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_21-76">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elisseeff_2000_296_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-elisseeff_2000_296-80">[77]</a></sup> Shen Kuo asserted that the needle will point south but with a deviation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elisseeff_2000_296_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-elisseeff_2000_296-80">[77]</a></sup> stating "[the magnetic needles] are always displaced slightly east rather than pointing due south."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_21_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_21-76">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shen Kuo wrote that it was preferable to use the twenty-four-point rose instead of the old eight compass cardinal points — and the former was recorded in use for navigation shortly after Shen's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_22_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_22-6">[6]</a></sup> The preference of use for the twenty-four-point-rose compass may have arisen from Shen's finding of a more accurate <a href="/wiki/Meridian_(astronomy)" title="Meridian (astronomy)">astronomical meridian</a>, determined by his measurement between the pole star and true north;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_22_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_22-6">[6]</a></sup> however, it could also have been inspired by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geomantic" title="Geomantic">geomantic</a> beliefs and practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_22_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_22-6">[6]</a></sup> The book of the author <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Yu_(author)" title="Zhu Yu (author)">Zhu Yu</a>, the <i>Pingzhou Table Talks</i> published in 1119 (written from 1111 to 1117), was the first record of use of a compass for seafaring navigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hsu_1988_102_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-hsu_1988_102-79">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_4_part_1_279_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_4_part_1_279-81">[78]</a></sup> However, Zhu Yu's book recounts events back to 1086, when Shen Kuo was writing the <i>Dream Pool Essays</i>; this meant that in Shen's time the compass might have already been in navigational use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_4_part_1_279_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_4_part_1_279-81">[78]</a></sup> In any case, Shen Kuo's writing on magnetic compasses has proved invaluable for understanding China's earliest use of the compass for seafaring navigation.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeology">Archaeology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Archaeology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Song_Dynasty#Archaeology" title="Song Dynasty">Song Dynasty § Archaeology</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Chinese_archaeology" title="History of Chinese archaeology">History of Chinese archaeology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_archaeology" title="History of archaeology">History of archaeology</a></div>
