<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_bronze_zun.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2200" data-file-width="1604" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_bronze_zun.jpg/170px-CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_bronze_zun.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_bronze_zun.jpg/255px-CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_bronze_zun.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_bronze_zun.jpg/340px-CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_bronze_zun.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_bronze_zun.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shang_Dynasty#Gallery" title="Shang Dynasty">Bronzeware</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shang_Dynasty" title="Shang Dynasty">Shang Dynasty</a> (1600–1046 BC); Song era antiquarians and archeologists in search of antiques for reviving ancient rituals claimed to have found bronzewares dated as far back as the Shang era, which contained written inscriptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Many of Shen Kuo's contemporaries were interested in <a href="/wiki/Antiquarian" title="Antiquarian">antiquarian</a> pursuits of collecting old artworks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ebrey_2006_163_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-ebrey_2006_163-83">[80]</a></sup> They were also interested in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Chinese_archaeology" title="History of Chinese archaeology">archaeological</a> pursuits, although for rather different reasons than why Shen Kuo held an interest in <a href="/wiki/History_of_archaeology" title="History of archaeology">archaeology</a>. While Shen's educated <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucian</a> contemporaries were interested in obtaining ancient relics and antiques in order to revive their use in rituals, Shen was more concerned with how items from archeological finds were originally manufactured and what their functionality would have been, based on <a href="/wiki/Empirical_evidence" title="Empirical evidence">empirical evidence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fraser_haber_1986_227_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-fraser_haber_1986_227-84">[81]</a></sup> Shen Kuo criticized those in his day who reconstructed ancient ritual objects using only their imagination and not the tangible evidence from archeological digs or finds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fraser_haber_1986_227_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-fraser_haber_1986_227-84">[81]</a></sup> Shen also disdained the notion of others that these objects were products of the "sages" or the <a href="/wiki/Four_occupations" title="Four occupations">aristocratic class of antiquity</a>, rightfully crediting the items' manufacture and production to the common working people and artisans of previous eras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fraser_haber_1986_227_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-fraser_haber_1986_227-84">[81]</a></sup> Fraser and Haber write that Shen Kuo "advocated the use of an <a href="/wiki/Interdisciplinarity" title="Interdisciplinarity">interdisciplinary</a> approach to archaeology and practiced such an approach himself through his work in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_ferrous_metallurgy" title="History of ferrous metallurgy">metallurgy</a>, optics, and geometry in the study of ancient measures."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fraser_haber_1986_227_84-3"><a href="#cite_note-fraser_haber_1986_227-84">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>While working in the Bureau of Astronomy, Shen Kuo's interest in archaeology and old relics led him to reconstruct an armillary sphere from existing models as well as from ancient texts that could provide additional information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fraser_haber_1986_227_84-4"><a href="#cite_note-fraser_haber_1986_227-84">[81]</a></sup> Shen used ancient mirrors while conducting his optics experiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fraser_haber_1986_227_84-5"><a href="#cite_note-fraser_haber_1986_227-84">[81]</a></sup> He observed ancient weaponry, describing the <a href="/wiki/Sight_(device)" title="Sight (device)">scaled sight devices</a> on ancient <a href="/wiki/Crossbow" title="Crossbow">crossbows</a> and the ancients' production of swords with composite blades that had a midrib of <a href="/wiki/Wrought_iron" title="Wrought iron">wrought iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Decarburization" title="Decarburization">low-carbon</a> <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> while having two sharp edges of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carburization" title="Carburization">high-carbon</a> steel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fraser_haber_1986_227_84-6"><a href="#cite_note-fraser_haber_1986_227-84">[81]</a></sup> Being a knowledgeable musician, Shen also suggested suspending <a href="/wiki/Bianzhong" title="Bianzhong">an ancient bell</a> by using a hollow handle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fraser_haber_1986_227_84-7"><a href="#cite_note-fraser_haber_1986_227-84">[81]</a></sup> In his assessment of the carved <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">reliefs</a> of the ancient Zhuwei Tomb, Shen stated that the reliefs demonstrate genuine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Society_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Society of the Han dynasty">Han Dynasty era clothing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rudolph_1963_176_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-rudolph_1963_176-85">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>After unearthing an ancient crossbow device from a house's garden in Haichow, Jiangsu, Shen discovered that the cross-wire grid sighting device, marked in graduated measurements on the stock, could be used to calculate the height of a distant mountain in the same way that mathematicians could apply right-angle triangles to measure height.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_574_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_574-86">[83]</a></sup> Needham asserts Shen had discovered the survey device known as <a href="/wiki/Jacob%27s_staff" title="Jacob's staff">Jacob's staff</a>, which was not described elsewhere until the <a href="/wiki/Provence" title="Provence">Provençal</a> Jewish mathematician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Levi_ben_Gerson" title="Levi ben Gerson">Levi ben Gerson</a> (1288–1344) wrote of it in 1321.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_573_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_573-87">[84]</a></sup> Shen wrote that while viewing the whole of a mountain, the distance on the instrument was long, but while viewing a small part of the mountainside the distance was short due to the device's cross piece that had to be pushed further away from the observer's eye, with the graduation starting on the further end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_574_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_574-86">[83]</a></sup> He wrote that if one placed an arrow on the device and looked past its end, the degree of the mountain could be measured and thus its height could be calculated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_574_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_574-86">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TaihangMountain8.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2060" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/TaihangMountain8.jpg/170px-TaihangMountain8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/TaihangMountain8.jpg/255px-TaihangMountain8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/TaihangMountain8.jpg/340px-TaihangMountain8.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TaihangMountain8.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of the <a href="/wiki/Taihang_Mountains" title="Taihang Mountains">Taihang Mountains</a>, where Shen Kuo had his <a href="/wiki/Epiphany_(feeling)" title="Epiphany (feeling)">epiphany</a> about <a href="/wiki/Geomorphology" title="Geomorphology">geomorphology</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The ancient <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (384 BC–322 BC) wrote in his <i><a href="/wiki/Meteorology_(Aristotle)" title="Meteorology (Aristotle)">Meteorology</a></i> of how the earth had the potential for physical change, including the belief that all rivers and seas at one time did not exist where they were, and were dry. The Greek writer <a href="/wiki/Xenophanes" title="Xenophanes">Xenophanes</a> (570 BC–480 BC) wrote of how inland <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_(ocean)" title="Marine (ocean)">marine</a> <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossils</a> were evidence that massive periodic <a href="/wiki/Flood" title="Flood">flooding</a> had wiped out mankind several times in the past, but never wrote of land formation or shifting seashores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-desmond_1975_692-707_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-desmond_1975_692-707-88">[85]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Du_Yu" title="Du Yu">Du Yu</a> (222–285) a Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jin_Dynasty_(265%E2%80%93420)" title="Jin Dynasty (265–420)">Jin Dynasty</a> officer, believed that the land of hills would eventually be leveled into valleys and valleys would gradually rise to form hills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup> The Daoist alchemist <a href="/wiki/Ge_Hong" title="Ge Hong">Ge Hong</a> (284–364) wrote of the legendary immortal <a href="/wiki/Magu_(deity)" title="Magu (deity)">Magu</a>; in a written dialogue by Ge, Ma Gu described how what was once the Eastern Sea (i.e. <a href="/wiki/East_China_Sea" title="East China Sea">East China Sea</a>) had transformed into solid land where <a href="/wiki/Morus_(plant)" title="Morus (plant)">mulberry trees</a> grew, and would one day be filled with mountains and dry, dusty lands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup> The later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_Empire" title="Persian Empire">Persian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> scholar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_Rayh%C4%81n_al-B%C4%ABr%C5%ABn%C4%AB" title="Abū Rayhān al-Bīrūnī">Abū Rayhān al-Bīrūnī</a> (973–1048) hypothesized that <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">India</a> was once covered by the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> while observing rock formations at the mouths of rivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-salam_1984_179-213_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-salam_1984_179-213-91">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Likan_Bamboo_and_Rocks.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="834" data-file-width="682" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Likan_Bamboo_and_Rocks.jpg/170px-Likan_Bamboo_and_Rocks.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Likan_Bamboo_and_Rocks.jpg/255px-Likan_Bamboo_and_Rocks.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Likan_Bamboo_and_Rocks.jpg/340px-Likan_Bamboo_and_Rocks.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Likan_Bamboo_and_Rocks.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bamboo and rocks by <a href="/wiki/Li_Kan_(painter)" title="Li Kan (painter)">Li Kan</a> (1244–1320); using evidence of fossilized bamboo within China's dry northwestern climate zone, Shen Kuo hypothesized that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_change_(general_concept)" title="Climate change (general concept)">climates naturally shifted geographically over time</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>It was Shen Kuo who formulated a hypothesis about the process of land formation (<a href="/wiki/Geomorphology" title="Geomorphology">geomorphology</a>) based upon several observations as evidence. This included his observation of <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> shells in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geological" title="Geological">geological</a> stratum of a mountain hundreds of miles from the ocean. He inferred that the land was reshaped and formed by <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">erosion</a> of the mountains, uplift, and the deposition of <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">silt</a>, after observing strange natural erosions of the <a href="/wiki/Taihang_Mountains" title="Taihang Mountains">Taihang Mountains</a> and the Yandang Mountain near <a href="/wiki/Wenzhou" title="Wenzhou">Wenzhou</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_603_604_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_603_604-92">[89]</a></sup> He hypothesized that, with the inundation of silt, the land of the continent must have been formed over an enormous span of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_23_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_23-93">[90]</a></sup> While visiting the Taihang Mountains in 1074, Shen Kuo noticed strata of <a href="/wiki/Bivalvia" title="Bivalvia">bivalve</a> shells and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovoid" title="Ovoid">ovoid</a> rocks in a horizontal-running span through a cliff like a large belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_23_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_23-93">[90]</a></sup> Shen proposed that the cliff was once the location of an ancient seashore that by his time had shifted hundreds of miles east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_23_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_23-93">[90]</a></sup> Shen wrote that in the Zhiping reign period (1064–1067) a man of Zezhou unearthed an object in his garden that looked like a serpent or dragon, and after examining it, concluded the dead animal had apparently turned to "stone".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_618_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_618-94">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chan_2002_15_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-chan_2002_15-95">[92]</a></sup> The magistrate of <a href="/wiki/Jincheng" title="Jincheng">Jincheng</a>, Zheng Boshun, examined the creature as well, and noted the same scale-like markings that were seen on other marine animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_618_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_618-94">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chan_2002_15_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-chan_2002_15-95">[92]</a></sup> Shen Kuo likened this to the "stone crabs" found in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_618_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_618-94">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chan_2002_15_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-chan_2002_15-95">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shen also wrote that since petrified bamboos were found underground in a climatic area where they had never been known to be grown, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_change_(general_concept)" title="Climate change (general concept)">climate there must have shifted geographically over time</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_614_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_614-96">[93]</a></sup> Around the year 1080, Shen Kuo noted that a landslide on the bank of a large river near Yanzhou (modern <a href="/wiki/Yan%27an" title="Yan'an">Yan'an</a>) had revealed an open space several dozens of feet under the ground once the bank collapsed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_614_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_614-96">[93]</a></sup> This underground space contained hundreds of petrified bamboos still intact with roots and trunks, "all turned to stone" as Shen Kuo wrote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_614_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_614-96">[93]</a></sup> Shen Kuo noted that bamboos do not grow in Yanzhou, located in northern China, and he was puzzled during which previous dynasty the bamboos could have grown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_614_96-3"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_614-96">[93]</a></sup> Considering that damp and gloomy low places provide suitable conditions for the growth of bamboo, Shen deduced that the climate of Yanzhou must have fit that description in very ancient times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_614_96-4"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_614-96">[93]</a></sup> Although this would have intrigued many of his readers, the study of <a href="/wiki/Paleoclimatology" title="Paleoclimatology">paleoclimatology</a> in medieval China did not develop into an established discipline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_614_96-5"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_614-96">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Song dynasty philosopher <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Xi" title="Zhu Xi">Zhu Xi</a> (1130–1200) wrote of this curious natural phenomenon of fossils as well.  He was known to have read the works of Shen Kuo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chan_2002_15_95-3"><a href="#cite_note-chan_2002_15-95">[92]</a></sup> Shen's description of soil erosion and weathering predated that of <a href="/wiki/Georgius_Agricola" title="Georgius Agricola">Georgius Agricola</a> in his book of 1546, <i><a href="/wiki/De_Natura_Fossilium" title="De Natura Fossilium">De veteribus et novis metallis</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_604_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_604-97">[94]</a></sup> Furthermore, Shen's theory of sedimentary deposition predated that of <a href="/wiki/James_Hutton" title="James Hutton">James Hutton</a>, whose groundbreaking work was published in 1802 (considered the foundation of modern geology).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_604_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_604-97">[94]</a></sup>  Historian <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Needham" title="Joseph Needham">Joseph Needham</a> likened Shen's account to that of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scottish</a> scientist <a href="/wiki/Roderick_Murchison" title="Roderick Murchison">Roderick Murchison</a> (1792–1871), who was inspired to become a geologist after observing a providential landslide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_604_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_604-97">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meteorology">Meteorology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Meteorology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Double-Rainbow.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2036" data-file-width="4186" decoding="async" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Double-Rainbow.jpg/220px-Double-Rainbow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Double-Rainbow.jpg/330px-Double-Rainbow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Double-Rainbow.jpg/440px-Double-Rainbow.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Double-Rainbow.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shen accurately hypothesized that <a href="/wiki/Rainbow" title="Rainbow">rainbows</a> were caused by sunlight passing through rain droplets.</div></div></div>
<p>Early speculation and hypothesis pertaining to what is now known as <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorology</a> had a long tradition in China before Shen Kuo. For example, the Han dynasty scholar <a href="/wiki/Wang_Chong" title="Wang Chong">Wang Chong</a> (27–97) accurately described the process of the <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[95]</a></sup> However, Shen made some observations that were not found elsewhere in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_literature" title="Chinese literature">Chinese literature</a>. For instance, Shen was the first in East Asia to describe <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornadoes</a>, which were thought to exist only in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_hemisphere" title="Western hemisphere">Western hemisphere</a> until their observation in China during the first decade of the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shen gave reasoning (earlier proposed by Sun Sikong, 1015–1076) that <a href="/wiki/Rainbow" title="Rainbow">rainbows</a> were formed by the shadow of the sun in rain, occurring when the sun would shine upon it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_24_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_24-100">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1984_534_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1984_534-101">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[99]</a></sup> Paul Dong writes that Shen's explanation of the rainbow as a phenomenon of <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_refraction" title="Atmospheric refraction">atmospheric refraction</a> "is basically in accord with modern scientific principles."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dong_2000_72_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-dong_2000_72-103">[100]</a></sup>  In Europe, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Bacon" title="Roger Bacon">Roger Bacon</a> (1214–1294) was the first to suggest that the colors of the rainbow were caused by the reflection and refraction of sunlight through rain drops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sarkar_salazar-palma_sengupta_2006_21_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-sarkar_salazar-palma_sengupta_2006_21-74">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shen hypothesized that rays of sunlight refract before reaching the surface of the earth, hence people on earth observing the sun are not viewing it in its exact position, in other words, the altitude of the apparent sun is higher than the actual altitude of the sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dong_2000_72_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-dong_2000_72-103">[100]</a></sup> Dong writes that "at the time, this discovery was remarkably original."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dong_2000_72_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-dong_2000_72-103">[100]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Haytham" title="Ibn al-Haytham">Ibn al-Haytham</a>, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Optics" title="Book of Optics">Book of Optics</a></i> (1021), also discussed atmospheric refraction in regard to <a href="/wiki/Twilight" title="Twilight">twilight</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sarkar_salazar-palma_sengupta_2006_21_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-sarkar_salazar-palma_sengupta_2006_21-74">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy_and_instruments">Astronomy and instruments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Astronomy and instruments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_astronomy" title="Chinese astronomy">Chinese astronomy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="708" data-file-width="984" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG/220px-Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG/330px-Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG/440px-Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of the five <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Star_map" title="Star map">star maps</a> published in 1092 AD for <a href="/wiki/Su_Song" title="Su Song">Su Song</a>'s horological and astronomical treatise, featuring Shen Kuo's corrected position of the <a href="/wiki/Pole_star" title="Pole star">pole star</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Being the head official for the Bureau of Astronomy, Shen Kuo was an avid scholar of medieval astronomy, and improved the designs of several astronomical instruments. Shen is credited with making improved designs of the <a href="/wiki/Gnomon" title="Gnomon">gnomon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armillary_sphere" title="Armillary sphere">armillary sphere</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Water_clock" title="Water clock">clepsydra</a> clock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_17_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_17-104">[101]</a></sup> For the clepsydra he designed a new overflow-tank type, and argued for a more efficient higher-order <a href="/wiki/Interpolation" title="Interpolation">interpolation</a> instead of linear interpolation in calibrating the measure of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_17_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_17-104">[101]</a></sup> Improving the 5th century model of the astronomical <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sighting_tube&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sighting tube (page does not exist)">sighting tube</a>, Shen Kuo widened its diameter so that the new calibration could observe the <a href="/wiki/Pole_star" title="Pole star">pole star</a> indefinitely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_17_104-2"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_17-104">[101]</a></sup> This came about due to the position of the pole star shifting in position since the time of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zu_Geng_(mathematician)" title="Zu Geng (mathematician)">Zu Geng</a> in the 5th century, hence Shen Kuo diligently observed the course of the pole star for three months, plotting the data of its course and coming to the conclusion that it had shifted slightly over three degrees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_17_104-3"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_17-104">[101]</a></sup> Apparently this astronomical finding had an impact upon the intellectual community in China at the time. Even Shen's political rival and contemporary astronomer <a href="/wiki/Su_Song" title="Su Song">Su Song</a> featured Shen's corrected position of the pole star (halfway between Tian shu, at −350 degrees, and the current <a href="/wiki/Polaris" title="Polaris">Polaris</a>) in the fourth <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Star_map" title="Star map">star map</a> of his celestial atlas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_278_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_278-105">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>The astronomical phenomena of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">solar eclipse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipse" title="Lunar eclipse">lunar eclipse</a> had been observed in the 4th century BC by astronomers <a href="/wiki/Gan_De" title="Gan De">Gan De</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shi_Shen" title="Shi Shen">Shi Shen</a>; the latter gave instructions on predicting the eclipses based on the relative position of the Moon to the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_411_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_411-106">[103]</a></sup> The philosopher Wang Chong argued against the 'radiating influence' theory of <a href="/wiki/Jing_Fang" title="Jing Fang">Jing Fang</a>'s writing in the 1st century BC and that of <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Heng" title="Zhang Heng">Zhang Heng</a> (78–139); the latter two correctly hypothesized that the brightness of the Moon was merely light reflected from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_413_414_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_413_414-107">[104]</a></sup> Jing Fang had written in the 1st century BC of how it was long accepted in China that the Sun and Moon were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spherical" title="Spherical">spherical</a> in shape ('like a <a href="/wiki/Crossbow" title="Crossbow">crossbow</a> bullet'), not flat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_3_227_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_3_227-108">[105]</a></sup> Shen Kuo also wrote of solar and lunar eclipses in this manner, yet expanded upon this to explain why the celestial bodies were spherical, going against the '<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flat_earth" title="Flat earth">flat earth</a>' theory for celestial bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_415_416_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_415_416-109">[106]</a></sup> However, there is no evidence to suggest that Shen Kuo supported a round earth theory, which was introduced into Chinese science by <a href="/wiki/Matteo_Ricci" title="Matteo Ricci">Matteo Ricci</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xu_Guangqi" title="Xu Guangqi">Xu Guangqi</a> in the 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fan_1996_431_432_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-fan_1996_431_432-110">[107]</a></sup> When the Director of the Astronomical <a href="/wiki/Observatory" title="Observatory">Observatory</a> asked Shen Kuo if the shapes of the Sun and Moon were round like balls or flat like fans, Shen Kuo explained that celestial bodies were spherical because of knowledge of waxing and waning of the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_415_416_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_415_416-109">[106]</a></sup> Much like what Zhang Heng had said, Shen Kuo likened the Moon to a ball of silver, which does not produce light, but simply reflects light if provided from another source (the Sun).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_415_416_109-2"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_415_416-109">[106]</a></sup> He explained that when the Sun's light is slanting, the Moon appears full.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_415_416_109-3"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_415_416-109">[106]</a></sup> He then explained if one were to cover any sort of sphere with white powder, and then viewed from the side it would appear to be a crescent, hence he reasoned that celestial bodies were spherical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dong_2000_72_103-3"><a href="#cite_note-dong_2000_72-103">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_415_416_109-4"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_415_416-109">[106]</a></sup> He also wrote that, although the Sun and Moon were in conjunction and opposition with each other once a month, this did not mean the Sun would be eclipsed every time their paths met, because of the small obliquity of their orbital paths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_415_416_109-5"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_415_416-109">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dong_2000_71_72_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-dong_2000_71_72-111">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clock_Tower_from_Su_Song%27s_Book_desmear.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1366" data-file-width="908" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Clock_Tower_from_Su_Song%27s_Book_desmear.JPG/170px-Clock_Tower_from_Su_Song%27s_Book_desmear.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Clock_Tower_from_Su_Song%27s_Book_desmear.JPG/255px-Clock_Tower_from_Su_Song%27s_Book_desmear.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Clock_Tower_from_Su_Song%27s_Book_desmear.JPG/340px-Clock_Tower_from_Su_Song%27s_Book_desmear.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clock_Tower_from_Su_Song%27s_Book_desmear.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The original diagram of <a href="/wiki/Su_Song" title="Su Song">Su Song</a>'s book of 1092 showing the inner workings of his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clocktower" title="Clocktower">clocktower</a>; a <a href="/wiki/Escapement" title="Escapement">mechanically rotated</a> <a href="/wiki/Armillary_sphere" title="Armillary sphere">armillary sphere</a> crowns the top.</div></div></div>
<p>Shen is also known for his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmological" title="Cosmological">cosmological</a> hypotheses in explaining the variations of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_motion" title="Planetary motion">planetary motions</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Apparent_retrograde_motion" title="Apparent retrograde motion">retrogradation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_16_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_16-112">[109]</a></sup> His colleague Wei Pu realized that the old calculation technique for the mean Sun was inaccurate compared to the <a href="/wiki/Solar_time" title="Solar time">apparent Sun</a>, since the latter was ahead of it in the accelerated phase of motion, and behind it in the retarded phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_19_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_19-113">[110]</a></sup> Shen's hypotheses were similar to the concept of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epicycle" title="Epicycle">epicycle</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Roman" title="Greco-Roman">Greco-Roman</a> tradition,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_16_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_16-112">[109]</a></sup> only Shen compared the side-section of <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbital</a> paths of planets and variations of planetary speeds to points in the tips of a <a href="/wiki/Willow" title="Willow">willow</a> leaf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_II_71-72_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_II_71-72-114">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-henderson_1986_128_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-henderson_1986_128-115">[112]</a></sup> In a similar rudimentary physical analogy of celestial motions, as John B. Henderson describes it, Shen likened the relationship of the Moon's path to the ecliptic, the path of the Sun, "to the figure of a rope coiled about a tree."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-henderson_1986_128_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-henderson_1986_128-115">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Along with his colleague <a href="/wiki/Wei_Pu" title="Wei Pu">Wei Pu</a> in the Bureau of Astronomy, Shen Kuo planned to plot out the exact coordinates of planetary and lunar movements by recording their astronomical observations three times a night for a continuum of five years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_18_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_18-8">[8]</a></sup> The Song astronomers of Shen's day still retained the lunar theory and coordinates of the earlier <a href="/wiki/Yi_Xing" title="Yi Xing">Yi Xing</a>, which after 350 years had devolved into a state of considerable error.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_18_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_18-8">[8]</a></sup> Shen criticized earlier Chinese astronomers for failing to describe celestial movement in spatial terms, yet he did not attempt to provide any reasoning for the motive power of the planets or other celestial movements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-henderson_1986_128_115-2"><a href="#cite_note-henderson_1986_128-115">[112]</a></sup> Shen and Wei began astronomical observations for the Moon and planets by plotting their locations three times a night for what should have been five successive years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_18_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_18-8">[8]</a></sup> The officials and astronomers at court were deeply opposed Wei and Shen's work, offended by their insistence that the coordinates of the renowned Yi Xing were inaccurate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_18_19_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_18_19-116">[113]</a></sup> They also slandered Wei Pu, out of resentment that a commoner had expertise exceeding theirs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_II_73_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_II_73-117">[114]</a></sup> When Wei and Shen made a public demonstration using the gnomon to prove the doubtful wrong, the other ministers reluctantly agreed to correct the lunar and solar errors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_18_19_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_18_19-116">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_II_72_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_II_72-118">[115]</a></sup> Despite this success, they eventually dismissed Wei and Shen's tables of planetary motions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_7_24-7"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_7-24">[22]</a></sup> Therefore, only the worst and most obvious planetary errors were corrected, and many inaccuracies remained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_II_73_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_II_73-117">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Movable_type_printing">Movable type printing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Movable type printing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_typography_in_East_Asia" title="History of typography in East Asia">History of typography in East Asia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jingangjing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3438" data-file-width="4917" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Jingangjing.jpg/220px-Jingangjing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Jingangjing.jpg/330px-Jingangjing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Jingangjing.jpg/440px-Jingangjing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jingangjing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Chinese <i><a href="/wiki/Diamond_Sutra" title="Diamond Sutra">Diamond Sutra</a></i>, the oldest known printed book in world history (868), using <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">woodblock printing</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Shen Kuo wrote that during the Qingli reign period (1041–1048), under <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Renzong_of_Song" title="Emperor Renzong of Song">Emperor Renzong of Song</a> (1022–1063), an obscure commoner and <a href="/wiki/Artisan" title="Artisan">artisan</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Bi_Sheng" title="Bi Sheng">Bi Sheng</a> (990–1051) invented ceramic <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">movable type</a> <a href="/wiki/Printing" title="Printing">printing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_201_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_201-119">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[117]</a></sup> Although the use of assembling individual characters to compose a piece of text had its origins in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">antiquity</a>, Bi Sheng's methodical innovation was something completely revolutionary for his time. Shen Kuo noted that the process was tedious if one only wanted to print a few copies of a book, but if one desired to make hundreds or thousands of copies, the process was incredibly fast and efficient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_201_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_201-119">[116]</a></sup> Beyond Shen Kuo's writing, however, nothing is known of Bi Sheng's life or the influence of movable type in his lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_202_203_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_202_203-121">[118]</a></sup> Although the details of Bi Sheng's life were scarcely known, Shen Kuo wrote:
</p>
<blockquote><p>When Bi Sheng died, his <a href="/wiki/Typeface" title="Typeface">fount of type</a> passed into the possession of my followers (i.e. one of Shen's nephews), among whom it has been kept as a precious possession until now.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_4_part_2_33_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_4_part_2_33-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_27_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_27-122">[119]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_movable_type_1313-ce.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1248" data-file-width="1247" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Chinese_movable_type_1313-ce.png/170px-Chinese_movable_type_1313-ce.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Chinese_movable_type_1313-ce.png/255px-Chinese_movable_type_1313-ce.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Chinese_movable_type_1313-ce.png/340px-Chinese_movable_type_1313-ce.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_movable_type_1313-ce.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A revolving table typecase with individual <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">movable type</a> characters arranged primarily by rhyming scheme, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wang_Zhen_(official)" title="Wang Zhen (official)">Wang Zhen</a>'s book of agriculture published in 1313.</div></div></div>
<p>There are a few surviving examples of books printed in the late Song Dynasty using movable type printing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu_1943_211_212_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wu_1943_211_212-123">[120]</a></sup> This includes Zhou Bida's <i>Notes of The Jade Hall</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">玉堂雜記</span></span>) printed in 1193 using the method of baked-clay movable type characters outlined in the <i>Dream Pool Essays</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[121]</a></sup> Yao Shu (1201–1278), an advisor to <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a>, once persuaded a disciple Yang Gu to print <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philological" title="Philological">philological</a> <a href="/wiki/Textbook" title="Textbook">primers</a> and Neo-Confucian texts by using what he termed the "movable type of Shen Kuo".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_203_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_203-125">[122]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wang_Zhen_(official)" title="Wang Zhen (official)">Wang Zhen</a> (fl. 1290–1333), who wrote the valuable agricultural, scientific, and technological treatise of the <i>Nong Shu</i>, mentioned an alternative method of baking <a href="/wiki/Earthenware" title="Earthenware">earthenware</a> type with earthenware frames in order to make whole blocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_203_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_203-125">[122]</a></sup> Wang Zhen also improved its use by inventing wooden movable type in the years 1297 or 1298, while he was a magistrate of <a href="/wiki/Jingde_County" title="Jingde County">Jingde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">Anhui</a> province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_206_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_206-126">[123]</a></sup> The earlier Bi Sheng had experimented with wooden movable type,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_205-206_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_205-206-127">[124]</a></sup> but Wang's main contribution was improving the speed of typesetting with simple mechanical devices, along with the complex, systematic arrangement of wooden movable type involving the use of revolving tables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_208_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_208-128">[125]</a></sup> Although later metal movable type would be used in China, Wang Zhen experimented with <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> metal movable type, but found its use to be inefficient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_217_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_217-129">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the 15th century, metal movable type printing was developed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming Dynasty</a> China (and earlier in <a href="/wiki/Goryeo" title="Goryeo">Goryeo</a> <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, by the mid 13th century), and was widely applied in China by at least the 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_211_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_211-130">[127]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a>, wealthy Ming era families sponsored the use of metal type printing (mostly using <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a>). This included the printing works of <a href="/wiki/Hua_Sui" title="Hua Sui">Hua Sui</a> (1439–1513), who pioneered the first Chinese bronze-type movable printing in the year 1490.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_212_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_212-131">[128]</a></sup> In 1718, during the mid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qing_Dynasty" title="Qing Dynasty">Qing Dynasty</a> (1644–1912), the scholar of <a href="/wiki/Tai%27an" title="Tai'an">Tai'an</a> known as Xu Zhiding developed movable type with <a href="/wiki/Vitreous_enamel" title="Vitreous enamel">enamelware</a> instead of earthenware.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_203_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_203-125">[122]</a></sup> There was also Zhai Jinsheng (b. 1784), a teacher of <a href="/wiki/Jing_County,_Anhui" title="Jing County, Anhui">Jingxian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">Anhui</a>, who spent thirty years making a font of earthenware movable type, and by 1844 he had over 100,000 Chinese writing characters in five sizes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_203_125-3"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_203-125">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite these advances, movable type printing never gained the amount of widespread use in East Asia that <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">woodblock printing</a> had achieved since the Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" title="Tang Dynasty">Tang Dynasty</a> in the 9th century. With <a href="/wiki/Written_Chinese" title="Written Chinese">written Chinese</a>, the vast amount of written <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morpheme</a> characters impeded movable type's acceptance and practical use, and was therefore seen as largely unsatisfactory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_201_119-2"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_201-119">[116]</a></sup> Furthermore, the European <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a>, first invented by <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Gutenberg" title="Johannes Gutenberg">Johannes Gutenberg</a> (1398–1468), was eventually wholly adopted as the standard in China, yet the tradition of woodblock printing remains popular in East Asian countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_201_119-3"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_5_part_1_201-119">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_achievements_in_science_and_technology">Other achievements in science and technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Other achievements in science and technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Shen Kuo described the phenomena of natural <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator" title="Predator">predator</a> <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> controlling the population of pests, the latter of which had the potential to wreak havoc upon the agricultural base of China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_6_part_1_545_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_6_part_1_545-132">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shen also wrote about <a href="/wiki/History_of_metallurgy_in_China" title="History of metallurgy in China">advancements in metallurgy</a>. While visiting the iron-producing district at Cizhou in 1075, Shen described the "partial <a href="/wiki/Decarburization" title="Decarburization">decarburization</a>" method of reforging cast iron under a cold blast, which Hartwell, Needham, and Wertime state is the predecessor of the <a href="/wiki/Bessemer_process" title="Bessemer process">Bessemer process</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hartwell_1966_54_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-hartwell_1966_54-133">[130]</a></sup> Shen was worried about <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[d]</a></sup> due to the needs of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_Song_Dynasty" title="Economy of the Song Dynasty">iron industry</a> and ink makers using pine soot in the production process, so he suggested for the latter an alternative of <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>, which he believed was "produced inexhaustibly within the earth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_24_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_24-100">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[131]</a></sup> Shen used the soot from the smoke of burned petroleum fuel (石油 <i>Shíyóu</i>, "rock oil" as Shen called it) to invent a new, more durable type of writing ink; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming Dynasty</a> pharmacologist <a href="/wiki/Li_Shizhen" title="Li Shizhen">Li Shizhen</a> (1518–1593) wrote that Shen's ink was "lustrous like <a href="/wiki/Lacquer" title="Lacquer">lacquer</a>, and superior to that made from pinewood lamp-black," or the soot from pinewood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beliefs_and_philosophy">Beliefs and philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Beliefs and philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">Chinese philosophy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Half_Portraits_of_the_Great_Sage_and_Virtuous_Men_of_Old_-_Meng_Ke_(%E5%AD%9F%E8%BB%BB).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1595" data-file-width="1178" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Half_Portraits_of_the_Great_Sage_and_Virtuous_Men_of_Old_-_Meng_Ke_%28%E5%AD%9F%E8%BB%BB%29.jpg/170px-Half_Portraits_of_the_Great_Sage_and_Virtuous_Men_of_Old_-_Meng_Ke_%28%E5%AD%9F%E8%BB%BB%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Half_Portraits_of_the_Great_Sage_and_Virtuous_Men_of_Old_-_Meng_Ke_%28%E5%AD%9F%E8%BB%BB%29.jpg/255px-Half_Portraits_of_the_Great_Sage_and_Virtuous_Men_of_Old_-_Meng_Ke_%28%E5%AD%9F%E8%BB%BB%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Half_Portraits_of_the_Great_Sage_and_Virtuous_Men_of_Old_-_Meng_Ke_%28%E5%AD%9F%E8%BB%BB%29.jpg/340px-Half_Portraits_of_the_Great_Sage_and_Virtuous_Men_of_Old_-_Meng_Ke_%28%E5%AD%9F%E8%BB%BB%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Half_Portraits_of_the_Great_Sage_and_Virtuous_Men_of_Old_-_Meng_Ke_(%E5%AD%9F%E8%BB%BB).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ideas of the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Mencius" title="Mencius">Mencius</a> deeply influenced Shen.</div></div></div>
<p>Shen Kuo was much in favor of philosophical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daoist" title="Daoist">Daoist</a> notions which challenged the authority of empirical science in his day. Although much could be discerned through empirical observation and recorded study, Daoism asserted that the secrets of the universe were boundless, something that scientific investigation could merely express in fragments and partial understandings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ropp_1990_170_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-ropp_1990_170-138">[134]</a></sup> Shen Kuo referred to the ancient Daoist <i><a href="/wiki/I_Ching" title="I Ching">I Ching</a></i> in explaining the spiritual processes and attainment of foreknowledge that cannot be attained through "crude traces", which he likens to mathematical astronomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ropp_1990_170_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-ropp_1990_170-138">[134]</a></sup> Nathan Sivin proposes that Shen was the first in history to "make a clear distinction between our unconnected experiences and the unitary causal world we postulate to explain them," which Biderman and Scharfstein state is arguably inherent in the works of <a href="/wiki/Heraclitus" title="Heraclitus">Heraclitus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a> as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[135]</a></sup> Shen was a firm believer in destiny and prognostication, and made rational explanations for the relations between them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_34_35_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_34_35-140">[136]</a></sup> Shen held a special interest in fate, mystical divination, bizarre phenomena, yet warned against the tendency to believe that all matters in life were preordained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_35_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_35-141">[137]</a></sup> When describing an event where <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a> had struck a house and all the wooden walls did not burn (but simply turned black) and <a href="/wiki/Lacquerware" title="Lacquerware">lacquerwares</a> inside were fine, yet metal objects had melted into liquid, Shen Kuo wrote:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Most people can only judge of things by the experiences of ordinary life, but phenomena outside the scope of this are really quite numerous. How insecure it is to investigate natural principles using only the light of common knowledge, and subjective ideas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_3_482_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_3_482-142">[138]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In his commentary on the ancient Confucian philosopher <a href="/wiki/Mencius" title="Mencius">Mencius</a> (372–289 BC), Shen wrote of the importance of choosing to follow what one knew to be a true path, yet the heart and mind could not attain full knowledge of truth through mere sensory experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_34_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_34-73">[70]</a></sup> In his own unique way but using terms influenced by the ideas of Mencius, Shen wrote of an autonomous inner authority that formed the basis for one's inclination towards moral choices, a concept linked to Shen's life experiences of surviving and obtaining success through self-reliance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_34_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_34-73">[70]</a></sup> Along with his commentary on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_classic_texts" title="Chinese classic texts">Chinese classic texts</a>, Shen Kuo also wrote extensively on the topics of supernatural <a href="/wiki/Divination" title="Divination">divination</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Buddhism" title="Chinese Buddhism">Buddhist</a> <a href="/wiki/Meditation" title="Meditation">meditation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ebrey_1999_148_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-ebrey_1999_148-143">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_criticism">Art criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Art criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dong_Yuan_Mountain_Hall.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4707" data-file-width="2675" decoding="async" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Dong_Yuan_Mountain_Hall.jpg/170px-Dong_Yuan_Mountain_Hall.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Dong_Yuan_Mountain_Hall.jpg/255px-Dong_Yuan_Mountain_Hall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Dong_Yuan_Mountain_Hall.jpg/340px-Dong_Yuan_Mountain_Hall.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dong_Yuan_Mountain_Hall.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A painting by <a href="/wiki/Dong_Yuan" title="Dong Yuan">Dong Yuan</a>, who Shen praised for his ability to portray landscapes and natural scenery in a grand but realistic style.</div></div></div>
<p>As an <a href="/wiki/Art_critic" title="Art critic">art critic</a>, Shen criticized the paintings of <a href="/wiki/Li_Cheng_(painter)" title="Li Cheng (painter)">Li Cheng</a> (919–967) for failing to observe the principle of "<i>seeing the small from the viewpoint of the large</i>" in portraying buildings and the like.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_4_115_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_4_115-144">[140]</a></sup> He praised the works of <a href="/wiki/Dong_Yuan" title="Dong Yuan">Dong Yuan</a> (c. 934–c. 962); he noted that although a close-up view of Dong's work would create the impression that his brush techniques were cursory, seen from afar his landscape paintings would give the impression of grand, resplendent, and realistic scenery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barnhart_1970_25_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-barnhart_1970_25-146">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[143]</a></sup> In addition, Shen's writing on Dong's artworks represents the earliest known reference to the <a href="/wiki/Jiangnan" title="Jiangnan">Jiangnan</a> style of painting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barnhart_1970_24_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-barnhart_1970_24-148">[144]</a></sup> In his "Song on Painting" and in his <i>Dream Pool Essays</i>, Shen praised the creative artworks of the Tang painter <a href="/wiki/Wang_Wei_(Tang_dynasty)" title="Wang Wei (Tang dynasty)">Wang Wei</a> (701–761); Shen noted that Wang was unique in that he "penetrated into the mysterious reason and depth of creative activity," but was criticized by others for not conforming his paintings to reality, such as his painting with a banana tree growing in a snowy, wintry landscape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li_1965_37_38_footnote_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-li_1965_37_38_footnote-149">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Written_works">Written works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Written works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Much of Shen Kuo's written work was probably purged under the leadership of minister <a href="/wiki/Cai_Jing" title="Cai Jing">Cai Jing</a> (1046–1126), who revived the New Policies of Wang Anshi, although he set out on a campaign of attrition to destroy or radically alter the written work of his predecessors and especially Conservative enemies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dengyuan_cited_in_sivin_1995_III_44_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dengyuan_cited_in_sivin_1995_III_44-152">[148]</a></sup> For example, only six of Shen's books remain, and four of these have been significantly altered since the time they were penned by the author.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_44_45_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_44_45-153">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>In modern times, the best attempt at a complete list and summary of Shen's writing was an appendix written by Hu Daojing in his standard edition of <i>Brush Talks</i>, written in 1956.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_44_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_44-154">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dream_Pool_Essays"><i>Dream Pool Essays</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Dream Pool Essays">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dream_Pool_Essays" title="Dream Pool Essays">Dream Pool Essays</a></div>
<p>Shen Kuo's <i>Dream Pool Essays</i> consists of some 507 separate essays exploring a wide range of subjects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bodde_1991_86_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-bodde_1991_86-155">[151]</a></sup> It was Shen's ultimate attempt to comprehend and describe a multitude of various aspects of nature, science, and reality, and all the practical and profound curiosities found in the world. The literal translation of the title, <i>Dream Brook Brush Talks</i>, refers to his Dream Brook estate, where he spent the last years of his life. About the title, he is quoted as saying: "Because I had only my writing brush and ink slab to converse with, I call it Brush Talks."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[e]</a></sup>
</p><p>The book was originally 30 chapters long, yet an unknown Chinese author's edition of 1166 edited and reorganized the work into 26 chapters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_45_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_45-157">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_written_works">Other written works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Other written works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Su_shi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1766" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="518" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Su_shi.jpg/220px-Su_shi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Su_shi.jpg/330px-Su_shi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Su_shi.jpg/440px-Su_shi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Su_shi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Poet and statesman <a href="/wiki/Su_Shi" title="Su Shi">Su Shi</a>, whose pharmaceutical work was combined with Shen Kuo's in 1126, in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuan_Dynasty" title="Yuan Dynasty">Yuan Dynasty</a> portrait by <a href="/wiki/Zhao_Mengfu" title="Zhao Mengfu">Zhao Mengfu</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Although the <i>Dream Pool Essays</i> is certainly his most extensive and important work, Shen Kuo wrote other books as well.  In 1075, Shen Kuo wrote the <i>Xining Fengyuan Li</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">熙寧奉元曆</span></span>; <i>The Oblatory Epoch astronomical system of the Splendid Peace reign period</i>), which was lost, but listed in a 7th chapter of a Song Dynasty bibliography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_46_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_46-158">[153]</a></sup>  This was the official report of Shen Kuo on his reforms of the Chinese calendar, which were only partially adopted by the Song court's official calendar system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_46_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_46-158">[153]</a></sup>  During his years of retirement from governmental service, Shen Kuo compiled a <a href="/wiki/Formulary_(pharmacy)" title="Formulary (pharmacy)">formulary</a> known as the <i>Liang Fang</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">良方</span></span>; <i>Good medicinal formulas</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_47_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_47-159">[154]</a></sup> Around the year 1126 it was combined with a similar collection by the famous <a href="/wiki/Su_Shi" title="Su Shi">Su Shi</a> (1037–1101), who was ironically a political opponent to Shen Kuo's faction of Reformers and New Policies supporters at court,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_47_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_47-159">[154]</a></sup> yet it was known that Shen Kuo and Su Shi were nonetheless friends and associates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1986_volume_1_137_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1986_volume_1_137-160">[155]</a></sup> Shen wrote the <i>Mengqi Wanghuai Lu</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">夢溪忘懷錄</span></span>; <i>Record of longings forgotten at Dream Brook</i>), which was also compiled during Shen's retirement.  This book was a treatise in the working since his youth on rural life and ethnographic accounts of living conditions in the isolated mountain regions of China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_48_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_48-161">[156]</a></sup>  Only quotations of it survive in the <i>Shuo Fu</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">說郛</span></span>) collection, which mostly describe the agricultural implements and tools used by rural people in high mountain regions.  Shen Kuo also wrote the <i>Changxing Ji</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">長興集</span></span>; <i>Collected Literary Works of [the Viscount of] Changxing</i>).  However, this book was without much doubt a posthumous collection, including various poems, prose, and administrative documents written by Shen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_48_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_48-161">[156]</a></sup> By the 15th century (during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming Dynasty</a>), this book was reprinted, yet only the 19th chapter remained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_48_161-2"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_48-161">[156]</a></sup>  This chapter was reprinted in 1718, yet poorly edited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_48_161-3"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_48-161">[156]</a></sup>  Finally, in the 1950s the author Hu Daojing supplemented this small yet valuable work with additions of other scattered poems written by Shen, in the former's <i>Collection of Shen Kua's Extant Poetry</i> (Shanghai: Shang-hai Shu-tian, 1958).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivin_1995_III_48_161-4"><a href="#cite_note-sivin_1995_III_48-161">[156]</a></sup> In the tradition of the popular Song era literary category of 'travel record literature' ('youji wenxue'),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hargett_1985_67_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-hargett_1985_67-162">[157]</a></sup> Shen Kuo also wrote the <i>Register of What Not to Forget</i>, a <a href="/wiki/Travel_literature" title="Travel literature">traveler's guide</a> to what type of <a href="/wiki/Carriage" title="Carriage">carriage</a> is suitable for a journey, the proper foods one should bring, the special clothing one should bring, and many other items.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hargett_1985_71_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-hargett_1985_71-163">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his <i>Sequel to Numerous Things Revealed</i>, the Song author Cheng Dachang (1123–1195) noted that stanzas prepared by Shen Kuo for military victory celebrations were later written down and published by Shen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stock_1993_94_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-stock_1993_94-164">[159]</a></sup> This includes a short poem "Song of Triumph" by Shen Kuo, who uses the musical instrument <i><a href="/wiki/Huqin" title="Huqin">mawei huqin</a></i> ('horse-tail barbarian stringed instrument' or 'horse-tail fiddle'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[160]</a></sup>) of the northwestern <a href="/wiki/Inner_Asia" title="Inner Asia">Inner Asian</a> nomads as a metaphor for prisoners-of-war led by Song troops:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>:The <i>mawei huqin</i> followed the Han chariot,
</p><dl><dd>Its music sounding of complaint to the Khan.</dd>
<dd>Do not bend the bow to shoot the goose within the clouds,</dd>
<dd>The returning goose bears no letter.<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Shen Kuo<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stock_1993_94_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-stock_1993_94-164">[159]</a></sup></cite></div></dd></dl></blockquote>
<p>Historian Jonathan Stock notes that the bent bow described in the poem above represents the arched bow used to play the <i>huqin</i>, while the sound of the instrument itself represented the discontent expressed by the prisoners-of-war with their defeated <a href="/wiki/Khan_(title)" title="Khan (title)">khan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stock_1993_94_164-2"><a href="#cite_note-stock_1993_94-164">[159]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shen_Kua_sketch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="326" data-file-width="281" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Shen_Kua_sketch.jpg/220px-Shen_Kua_sketch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/09/Shen_Kua_sketch.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shen_Kua_sketch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sketch of Shen Kuo by an anonymous artist of the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a>, 18th century</div></div></div>
<h3><span id="Praise.2C_critique.2C_and_criticism"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Praise,_critique,_and_criticism">Praise, critique, and criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Praise, critique, and criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the <i>Routledge Curzon Encyclopedia of Confucianism</i>, Xinzhong Yao states that Shen Kuo's legacy was tainted by his eager involvement in Wang Anshi's New Policies reforms, his actions criticized in <a href="/wiki/Twenty-Four_Histories" title="Twenty-Four Histories">the later traditional histories</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yao_2003_544_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-yao_2003_544-1">[1]</a></sup> However, Shen's reputation as a polymath has been well regarded. The <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">British</a> sinologist, historian, and biochemist Joseph Needham (1900–1995) stated that Shen Kuo was "one of the greatest scientific minds in Chinese history."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[161]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a> sinologist <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Gernet" title="Jacques Gernet">Jacques Gernet</a> is of the opinion that Shen possessed an "amazingly modern mind."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gernet_1996_338_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-gernet_1996_338-167">[162]</a></sup> Yao states of Shen's thorough recording of natural sciences in his <i>Dream Pool Essays</i>:
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>We must regard Shen Kuo's collection as an indispensable primary source attesting to the unmatched level of attainment achieved by Chinese science prior to the twelfth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yao_2003_545_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-yao_2003_545-168">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>However, Toby E. Huff writes that Shen Kuo's "scattered set" of writings lacks clear-cut organization and "theoretical acuteness," that is, <a href="/wiki/Scientific_theory" title="Scientific theory">scientific theory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huff_2003_303_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-huff_2003_303-169">[164]</a></sup> Nathan Sivin wrote that Shen's originality stands "cheek by jowl with trivial didacticism, court anecdotes, and ephemeral curiosities" that provide little insight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huff_2003_303_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-huff_2003_303-169">[164]</a></sup> Donald Holzman writes that Shen "has nowhere organized his observations into anything like a general theory."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huff_2003_303_169-2"><a href="#cite_note-huff_2003_303-169">[164]</a></sup> Huff writes that this was a systemic problem of early Chinese science, which lacked systematic treatment that could be found in European works such as the <i>Concordance and Discordant Canons</i> by the lawyer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gratian_(jurist)" title="Gratian (jurist)">Gratian</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a> (fl. 12th century).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huff_2003_303_169-3"><a href="#cite_note-huff_2003_303-169">[164]</a></sup> In regard to an overarching concept of <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> which could branch together all the various sciences studied by the Chinese, Sivin asserts that the writings of Shen Kuo "do not indicate that he achieved, or even sought, an integrated framework for his diverse knowledge; the one common thread is the varied responsibilities of his career as a high civil servant."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Burial_and_posthumous_honors">Burial and posthumous honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Kuo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Burial and posthumous honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Upon his death, Shen Kuo was interred in a tomb in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuhang" title="Yuhang">Yuhang</a> District of <a href="/wiki/Hangzhou" title="Hangzhou">Hangzhou</a>, at the foot of the Taiping Hill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yuhang_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yuhang-171">[166]</a></sup> His tomb was eventually destroyed, yet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming Dynasty</a> records indicated its location, which was found in 1983 and protected by the government in 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yuhang_171-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yuhang-171">[166]</a></sup> The remnants of the tomb's brick structure remained, along with Song Dynasty <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glasswares</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">coins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yuhang_171-2"><a href="#cite_note-Yuhang-171">[166]</a></sup> The Hangzhou Municipal Committee completed a restoration of Shen's tomb in September 2001.
